The Committee were of opinion that it is not at present necessary to appoint an officer specially for inspection of meat, and recommended the Council—That the reference of the matter to them be discharged. Unsound Food Regulations (First Series.) On 12th September the Local Government Board issued regulations (which were published in the "London Gazette" on the 15th September) under the Public Health (Regulations as to Food) Act, 1907, for the purpose of preventing danger to the public health from importation, preparation, storage, and distribution of articles of food or drink other than drugs or water. The Regulations took effect on and after October 1st, but the Borough of Poplar was not included as one of the Sanitary Authorities under the Regulations The Local Government Board issued an "Order'' on the 29th December requiring the Port Sanitary Authority and the Poplar Borough Council to act together for the purposes of the Regulations. Object of Regulations. The object of these Regulations is to secure that articles of food which are unsound, unwholesome, or unfit for human consumption, and which, as part of the cargo of a ship, are brought to a place in this country either as a place of destination or as a place of deposit for transmission to a place of destination elsewhere in the United Kingdom so that articles of food may be dealt with at the port of discharge. Inspection. Powers of inspection for the purposes of the Regulations are conferred upon the Medical Officer of Health, or any duly qualified medical practitioner appointed or employed by the Sanitary Authority to act in the execution of the Regulations, and upon any other person appointed or employed by the Authority under the Regulations to exercise any powers or discharge any duties assigned to the Medical Officer of Health. Place and Method of Inspection. The Medical Officer of Health is authorised by Article IV. to examine articles of food which have been landed within the district of the Sanitary 151 Authority. If the circumstances, in his opinion, so require, he may also examine such articles before they have been landed. The same Article provides for necessary facilities being given for the purposes of his examination in the matter of convenient arrangement, unpacking, and the like, and Article VI. authorises him to apply to a Justice for a warrant to enter premises in special cases. Taking of Samples. In order to meet the case of particular consignments in which it is necessary that special examination should be made (e.g., in the laboratory), powers of sampling are conferred by Article VIII. Article VIII. also provides for the temporary detention of consignments pending the completion of the examination of the samples taken. Communication with Customs Officers. Article XIV. (2) provides that in certain circumstances the examination of an article of food by an officer of the Sanitary Authority shall not be made without the consent of the Officer of Customs. The Officer of Customs is, however, required to afford facilities for such examination. Procedure in connection ivith Unsound Food. Under Article IV. the Medical Officer of Health is empowered, himself or by an assistant, to seize and carry away an article of food which, in his opinion, is unsound or unwholesome, or unfit for human consumption, in order that application may be made to a Justice for an order of condemnation. Alternatively, the Medical Officer of Health, by notice to the importer or to any other person having charge of the article, may require that until it has been examined by a Justice it shall not be removed from the place specified in the notice without the permission of the Medical Officer of Health. Such notice, however, does not preclude the removal of the food in question, at the request or with the consent of the importer, and with the permission of the Medical Officer of Health, for the purpose of its destruction or for the application of a process which will effectually prevent the article from being used for human consumption. The Justice, if satisfied as regards the unsoundness of the particular food concerned, is required by Articles V. and VI. to condemn the food and order its destruction or its disposal under the supervision of the Medical Officer of Health by such means and in such a manner as to prevent its being used for human consumption. If, however, evidence 152 satisfactory to the Justice is adduced that the food in question, although unsound, is not intended for sale for human consumption, he is required to make an order dismissing the complaint. A description of the food in question is to be given in any such order of dismissal, and a copy of the order is to be furnished to the Sanitary Authority. Article VII. requires the Sanitary Authority to keep for a period of not less than twelve months a record of any article of food destroyed or disposed of in pursuance of the Regulations. Provision for Determination of Differences, etc. Article XIII. empowers the Board, on the application of all the parties affected, to determine, either as arbitrators or otherwise, any difference arising in relation to any subject-matter of, or anything done under, the Regulations. Foreign Meat Regulations. On ]2th September the Local Government Board issued Foreign Meat Regulations (which was published in the "London Gazette" on the 15th September) under the Public Health (Regulations as to Food) Act, 1907, for the purpose of preventing danger to the public health from importation, preparation, storage, and distribution of articles of food or drink other than drugs or water. The Regulations were to come into operation on the 1st day of January, 1909, but the Borough of Poplar was not included as one of the Sanitary Authorities under the Regulations. The Local Government Board issued an order on the 29th January requiring the Port Sanitary Authority and the Poplar Borough Council to act together for the purposes of the Regulations. Object of the Regulations. The Regulations are designed to deal with foreign boneless meat which is imported in the form of scraps, trimmings, or other pieces not sufficiently identifiable with definite parts of a carcase, and which has not before importation been made ready for human consumption in the form of a sausage, or of another prepared or manufactured article of food; with imported tripe, tongues, or kidneys to which certain chemical preservatives have been applied; and with severed parts of a pig not prepared before importation as bacon or ham, and not contained in a package with an "official certificate" of the kind referred to below unopened on it or attached to it. These meats are classed together in the Regulations as "Foreign Meat of Class I.," and unless ths importer of meat of this class 153 exports it at his own expense, or furnishes proof, in the manner prescribed by the Regulations, that it is not intended for sale for human consumption the meat is required to be destroyed. With a view to stopping the importation of carcases of pigs from which the lymphatic glands about the throat and elsewhere are absent (thereby preventing the detection of evidence of tuberculosis and other disease in the carcase), the Regulations provide that carcases of pigs, not prepared as bacon or ham, which are imported without the lymphatic glands, and from which the head has been removed (Foreign Meat of Class II.) shall be dealt with in a similar manner to Foreign Meat of Class I. Foreign Meat of Class III. consists of the severed parts of a pig, not prepared as bacon or ham, and not being Foreign Meat of Class I., but contained in a package with an "official certificate" attached as evidence that the pig from which the meat is derived has been certified by a competent authority in the place of origin to be free from disease at the time of slaughter, and that the meat has been certified by the like authority to have been dressed or prepared, and packed with the needful observance of all requirements for the prevention of danger arising to public health from the meat as an article of food. A further object of the Regulations is to provide means by which oreign meat generally which is diseased, unsound, unwholesome, or unfit or human consumption may be detected and dealt with by public health authorities at the place of importation. Date on luhich the Regulations take effect. The Regulations will take effect on and after 1st January, 1909. Inspection. Powers of inspection for the purposes of the Regulations are conferred upon the Medical Officer of Health, or any duly qualified medical practitioner appointed or employed by the Sanitary Authority to act in the execution of the Regulations, and upon any other person appointed or employed by the authority under the Regulations to exercise any powers or discharge any duties assigned to the Medical Officer of Health. The appointment and payment of such persons, with the consent of the Board, is authorised by Article XV. of the Regulations. 154 Procedure under the Regulations. The Regulations require by Article III. (2) that the Officer of Customs shall cause to be detained, for examination by the Medical Officer of Health of the Sanitary Authority, any foreign meat which according to the best opinion which, in the circumstances of the case, his knowledge enables him to form has the characteristics of foreign meat of either Class I. or Class II. If he finds that the cargo comprises Foreign Meat of Class III. or Foreign Meat Unclassed he may allow it to be removed unless he is of opinion that the meat requires examination by the Medical Officer of Health. It will be the duty of the Medical Officer of Health, on being informed by the Customs Officer of the detention, to examine the meat and to give either a notice forbidding its removal for any purpose other than exportation, or a certificate that it may be removed. But he is not to give a certificate and must give a notice as regards Foreign Meat of Class I. or Class II., and also Article X. requires a record to be kept of foreign meat destroyed, and Article XII. enables the Medical Officer of Health to take samples of foreign meat for the purpose of the Regulations. He is to dispose of the samples in such manner as the Sanitary Authority direct. It will be observed that under Article IV. the Medical Officer of Health is empowered to examine any foreign meat which is still in the district; even though its removal from the ship, or from the place of delivery or of landing, may have been allowed, and that if he is of opinion that it should not be removed to a place of destination for any purpose other than exportation, he is to give notice forbidding its removal except for exportation. Provision for determination of Differences, &c. *Article XVII. empowers the Board, on the application of all the parties affected, to determine, either as arbitrators or otherwise, any difference arising in relation to any subject-matter of, or anything done under, the Regulations. On the 12th December the Local Government Board issued a circular letter drawing attention to further matters in connection with the administration of the Public Health (Foreign Meat) Regulations. In connection with this circular letter specimen forms for use in connection with the Foreign Meat Regulations were appended. 155 Unsound Food (First Series) Regulations, 1908. Foreign Meat Regulations, 1908. London Port Sanitary Authority and Poplar Borough Council. On 4th December the Local Government Board forwarded the following letter with copies of their draft regulations to the Poplar Borough Council:— [copy.] 135,968 E. 1908. Local Government Board, Whitehall, S.W. 4th December, 1908. Sir,— I am directed by the Local Government Board to state that the Board have had under consideration the question of the application of the Public Health (Foreign Meat) Regulations, 1908, and the Public Health (First Series: Unsound Food) Regulations, 1908, in the case of foreign meat and food to be landed in the Metropolitan Borough of Poplar, and have caused Orders (drafts of which are enclosed) to be prepared under Articles XVI. and XII. of the Regulations respectively, requiring joint action by the Council of the Borough and the Port Sanitary Authority of the Port of London for the purposes of the Regulations in the matter. In the case of the Unsound Food Regulations, it is proposed that the powers and duties under those Regulations in regard to food landed in the Borough shall, except in the cases referred to in Paragraph 2, be exercised by the Council and their Medical Officer of Health. In cases where the Port Medical Officer of Health has specified a. place in the Borough for the detention of food, he is to notify the Medical Officer of Health of the Borough, who will thereupon deal with the food. Where food is consigned to a wharf in the Borough, has been examined by the Port Medical Officer of Health and found to be in good condition, he will, if the importer desires it, notify the Medical Officer of Health of the Borough of the fact of the examination, and no further examination will then be made by that Medical Officer of Health unless he has reason to think that the food has subsequently become unsound. 156 In the case of the Foreign Meat Regulations it is proposed that meat which, after it has been landed in the Borough is made the subject of notice by the Officer of Customs, the Council and their Medical Officer, except in the cases specified in Paragraph 3, shall deal with it. When the meat is consigned to a wharf in the Borough and has been examined by the Officer of Customs on board the ship that officer will give notice to the Port Medical Officer of Health, who will notify the Medical Officer of Health of the Borough, who will then deal with the meat. If the Port Medical Officer of Health has given a certificate releasing any foreign meat from detention under Sub-division (5) of Article III. of the Regulations, no further examination will then be made by the Medical Officer of Health of the Borough unless he has reason to believe that the meat has subsequently become unsound. As it is desirable that the orders should be issued as soon as may be practicable, the Board direct me to request that if the Council desire to make any observations respecting the proposed orders they may be forwarded by as early a date as possible. Copies of the Regulations to be applied by the proposed Orders are also enclosed. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, (Signed) H. C. MONRO, Assistant Secretary. The Town Clerk of the Metropolitan Borough of Poplar. Accommodation for Persons Displaced, The London County Council asked the Local Government Board to decide that a scheme for providing new dwellings to accommodate persons of the working classes to be displaced by certain school extensions was not necessary in view of housing provision already made by the Council and vacant accommodation in the neighbourhoods affected. Five houses in British Street and Cahir Street were proposed to be demolished and 42 persons would be displaced. The Local Government Board asked for the observations of the Council on the matter. The Public Health and Housing Committee were of opinion it was not necessary that special housing accommodation should be provided by the London County Council in respect of the premises in the Borough 159 proposed to be demolished, and recommended the Council to concur in this opinion, and the Local Government Board be so informed. The London County Council proposed to demolish Nos. 8 and 9, Coborn Street, for extension of Malmesbury Road School, and asked the Local Government Board to decide that a scheme for the provision of new dwellings to accommodate persons of the working class, who would be displaced, was unnecessary. The London County Council pointed out there were 600 rooms available within a quarter of a mile, and the 11 persons to be displaced would experience no difficulty in finding new accommodation. The Local Government Board forwarded copy of the application for the observations of the Borough Council. The Public Health and Housing Committee were of opinion a scheme for re-housing the persons to be displaced was unnecessary, and recommended the Council to concur in this opinion, and the Local Government Board be so informed. Proposed Sale of Housing Land (London County Council). Notice of motion was given to move— "That this Council emphatically protests against the proposal now before the London County Council to sell or lease to private persons 200 acres of land which have been acquired by past Councils for the purpose of housing the people, to so do being a gross breach of trust, the London County Council having no mandate from the people to do so. That copies of this resolution be forwarded to the London County Council, the members of the London County Council, and the President of the Local Government Board." National Housing Congress. Together with the Chairman of the Public Health and Housing Committee (Mr. Councillor A. H. Darby), the Medical Officer attended the National Housing Congress, held at the Westminster Palace Hotel, on the 4th and 5th May. The Congress was convened by the National Housing Reform Council, to discuss the provisions of the Housing and Town Planning Bill introduced into Parliament by the President of the Local Government Board. More than three hundred delegates and representatives attended the great Housing Congress to discuss the provisions of Mr. Burns's Housing 160 and Town Planning Bill. The measure was thoroughly discussed from every point of view, and important suggestions for amendment were made. The chair was occupied all day on Monday and on Tuesday afternoon by Sir John Dickson Poynder, Bart., M.P., Chairman of the Select Committee on Bural Housing, and on Tuesday morning by Alderman W. Thompson, Chairman of the National Housing Beform Council. Whilst the Congress approved of the general principles on which the Bill was founded it carried a large number of amendments. It almost unanimously approved of land purchase in connection with town planning schemes, and it was equally emphatic in supporting that to make it obligatory upon local authorities to obtain the consent of the Local Government Board before even proceeding with the preparation of a town plan is quite superfluous and can only produce unnecessary delay. The following resolution was moved and seconded:— "This Congress welcomes the removal of the restriction on the period of repayment of loans from the Public Works Loans Commissioners (Clause 3, b and c), but in view of the terms accorded to Irish local authorities (under the Irish Labourers Act, 1906), this Congress is of opinion that the Treasury should lend money for housing purposes at the lowest rate at which the Treasury can itself borrow: — (a) To local authorities up to the full amount of the security; (b) To public utility societies up to 75 per cent, of the security. The following amendment was moved:— "That in order to encourage local authorities to provide improved housing accommodation for the working classes, apart from street widenings, improvements, or town planning schemes, an amendment should be inserted providing that, subject to the approval of the Local Government Board to the plans, rents to be charged, scheme, and to a proper audit of the housing accounts, a grant should be made to cover either the whole, or such a portion as the Local Government Board may determine, of the annual deficiency (if any) arising upon each completed year's revenue account, and now chargeable to the local rates, upon the condition that when all outgoings have been defrayed, and a surplus arises the whole of such surplus shall be utilised each year in the repayment of the sums granted, without 161 interest, and that the Government should create a special Housing Fund for the purpose." After discussion, the amendment was put to the meeting and defeated, only 25 voting for it. A second amendment was moved in the following terms:— "That Clause 3 should be amended to provide that loans by Public Works Loans Commissioners shall be advanced, if required, upon application by any authority carrying out the provisions of this Act." This amendment was also defeated. A further amendment was moved as follows:— "That Clause 5 should provide that the expense incurred in the laying-out and the construction of any streets or roads may be made from the General or District Rate, but such contribution shall only be made after approval by the Local Government Board, and that this expense should also apply to, and power be given to local authorities to alter, widen, close, or deviate existing streets in an housing area." This amendment was likewise defeated. Another amendment was brought forward, which ran as follows:— "That after Par. (b) in Resolution I., the following words be added: "and that local authorities should be assisted by a subsidy, if necessary, from the Exchequer, to build houses under Part III. of the Housing of the Working Classes Act, at low rentals of from Is. 9d. to 3s. per week." The amendment was negatived, and the original resolution carried. The following resolution was moved:— "This Congress strongly approves of the provisions for the issue of closing and demolition orders by local authorities (Clauses 13 and 14), and further approves of Clauses 26 and 27 as giving a much needed power to local authorities to insist upon dwellings being kept 'reasonably fit for human habitation'; this Congress, however, urges, that Clause 26 should apply to all dwellings not exceeding £40 rental in London, and £26 rental elsewhere." 162 The resolution was carried. The Town Clerk of Edinburgh moved as an addendum that power should be sought such as they possessed in Edinburgh. On a show of hands this was lost by 64 to 63. The following addendum was moved and seconded:— "That local authorities shall have power to make a demolition order in cases where they have made a closing order against the property and that property has not been put into a sanitary condition within three months." This was agreed to. A third resolution was moved as follows:— "This Congress approves the provisions for securing— (a) Improved powers of entry for purposes of inspection (Clause 32); (b) The appointment by every County Council of a Medical Officer of Health under Section 17 of the Local Governmant Act; but this Congress is of opinion that a grant should be made from the central Exchequer to County and County Borough Councils to enable them, in co-operation with minor local authorities, to make a quinquennial survey and prepare a register of dwellings, in accordance with the unanimous recommendations of the Select Committee on Rural Housing (Report, pars 53 and 54)." The resolution, on being put, was carried unanimously. A fourth resolution was moved as follows:— "This Congress approves of the provisions which secure— (a) That Part III. of the principal Act may be put into operation without formal adoption ; (b) That improved procedure shall be established for the acquisition of land for Part III. schemes ; 163 (c) That County Councils are given additional powers (Clauses 11 and 58); but this Congress is of opinion— (1) That County Councils should be local authorities, with concurrent powers (as in the case of London), for the purposes of Part III. and for town planning ; (2) That the power to supply land with cottages under the Housing Acts should not be limited to a quantity 'not exceeding half an acre '; (3) That provision should be made for securing a more effective inter-working with the Small Holdings Act (1907).'' The main resolution having been carried by two to one, it was moved to add :— "That this Congress urges the Government to allow local authorities to prescribe bye-laws fixing the minimum cubic capacity of living and bed rooms in houses proposed to be built in their area, and also to adopt a bye-law to compel the adoption of a minimum area of at least 144 square feet in one room of every new house." The addendum was then adopted. A further addendum was moved:— " That as some local authorities allow their building bye-laws to be not carried out in their entirety, this Congress presses on the Government to take steps to secure the definite execution of such bye-laws, and that power be given to four inhabitants to call the attention of the Local Government Board to neglect in administration. This Congress is also of opinion that all local authorities should now be required to adopt bye-laws containing at least the minimum requirements of the model bye-laws of the Local Government Board." This was seconded and adopted, with the exception of the last sentence. The following addendum was moved "That this Congress is of opinion that in regard to the rural 164 districts, a clause should be inserted in the Bill to make the tenure of the labourer's cottage more secure than it is at present." The addendum was formally seconded and adopted. It was moved:— "That both rural and urban Medical Officers of Health should not be dismissed without the consent of the Local Government Board." Several delegates objected to the proposal, and the previous question was carried with practical unanimity. It was moved:— "This Congress welcomes the town planuing and land purchase clauses, but— (1) Strongly urges that the area in respect of which town planning schemes may be made should comprise all land within the area of local authorities or in the neighbourhood of their area, and should not be limited to 'land which appears likely to be used for building purposes '; (2) Is of opinion that the preliminary permission for a local authority to prepare town plans for land within its own area is not necessary. Further, this Congress is of opinion that the Local Government Board should prescribe provisions empowering local authorities— (a) To limit the number of houses or rooms to the acre; and (b) In exchange for the dedication by landowners of open spaces or other concessions beneficial to the health and amenity of the community, to relax limitation and to vary bye-laws or other conditions which prevent economical road construction and cottage building." It was moved, by way of amendment:— "To leave out V. (1) and (2), and substitute the following:— "This Congress, being of opinion that improvements in the laying-out and extension of towns can best be secured by enactments 165 applying to the provinces the principles of the London Building Acts, and by increasing the powers of local authorities to make appropriate bye-laws, especially as regards provision, when necessary, of facilities for the prolongation and connection of any new streets, sanctioned by such authorities, recommends that Part II. of the Bill be withdrawn, and that steps be taken as soon as possible for amending the law in the direction indicated." The amendment was then put, four voting for it and most of the rest against. Sections (1) and (2) of the resolution were then carried separately. The second part of the resolution [(a) and (b)] was then put and carried with two dissentients. It was then moved as an addendum:— "After the word 'acre' in Par. V. (Sub-sec. a of the resolution), to add and specify class of houses to be erected, to prohibit, if deemed desirable in the interest of the district, the erection of any factory, workshop, or business premises." The motion was then put, and defeated by 67 votes to 65. It was then moved:— "To add to the resolution— "(c) To compel every builder of a dwelling or dwellings to contribute without payment a proportion of his land for a playground or playgrounds and other open spaces, deemed necessary by the local authority, and for a street of the width decided on by the local authority, provided always that no builder shall be compelled to contribute without payment more land for these purposes than he would have been compelled by the bye-laws hitherto in force in the local authority to provide for his proportion of a street." Addendum was put, and carried by a large majority. A further addendum was moved in the following terms :— "That it is expedient that local authorities should be empowered to prepare and, subject to the approval of the Local Government Board, to enforce schemes for laying-out streets or roads and for m 166 securing sanitary conditions in the buildings to be erected upon the local authorities' area and in the neighbourhood thereof to which the schemes respectively apply without being compelled to acquire land or incur expenditure in forming the roads, drains, &c." The addendum was carried. An addendum was moved which ran as follows: "To add after Clause (a) and (b)" To require an open space in every given area." and on the words, "if not otherwise provided for" being inserted, it was carried. A final addendum to the resolution was moved as follows:— "Further, this Conference considers that a new sub-section should be added to Clause 50, providing that 'where the capital value of any land shall have been ascertained for purposes of taxation, the same shall be taken to be its value if purchased by any local authority, subject to such addition for severance or other special damage as the arbitrator may decide.' " The addendum was carried. The following resolution was moved : "That this Congress, recognising the vital importance to the nation of adequate housing administration, is of opinion that under the Bill definite duties should be placed upon the Local Government Board, as well as on the local authorities, and in order to secure the effective performance of such new duties a special housing department should be created, to inform, encourage and assist local authorities in their efforts for housing reform. "Further, this Congress is of opinion that greater liberty and freedom of initiative in constructive action should be granted by Parliament to local authorities." The resolution was carried with four dissentients. The following addendum was moved:— "That this Congress expresses its regret that the Government 167 have made the present Bill a Bill by way of reference, and hope that on an early date they will introduce a Bill to codify and simplify the Acts relating to the Housing of Working Classes." The addendum was carried. A further addendum in the following terms was moved :— "That this Congress is of opinion that local authorities should be given a right of appeal to Parliament against any order of the Local Government Board made under Clauses 12 and 51, and objected to by such local authority." On a show of hands being taken, the voting on the proposed addendum resulted in a tie—31 for and 31 against. The Chairman gave his easting vote against the addendum, which was accordingly defeated. The Chairman finally moved the following resolution:— "That, subject to the suggestions made in the resolutions passed, this Congress heartily welcomes the Housing Bill, and trusts that it will be passed into law during the present session of Parliament." The resolution was adopted. The Housing and Town Planning Bill. To amend the law relating to the housing of the working classes, to provide for the making of town planning schemes, and to make further provision with respect to the appointment and duties of county Medical Officers of Health, and to provide for the establishment of Public Health and Housing Committees of County Councils. Presented by Mr. Burns supported by Mr. Solicitor-General for Scotland, Mr. Attorney-General, and Mr. Masterman. Mr. Burns formally presented it for first reading on Wednesday, February 17th, 1909. There are four parts of the Bill. One part deals with the housing of the working classes, another with town planning, and the third makes further provision with respect to the appointment and duties of county Medical Officers of Health, and also provides for the establishment of Public Health and Housing Committees of County Councils. Included in the housing part of the Bill is a clause defining the duty of local authorities with regard to closing dwelling houses unfit for human 168 habitation. The duty is imposed on them to cause to be made from time to time an inspection of their district for the purpose of ascertaining whether any dwelling house therein is in a state so dangerous or injurious to health as to be unfit for human habitation. If on the representation of the Medical Officer of Health or of any officer of the authority, or on information given, it appears to them that any dwelling house is in such a state, it becomes their duty to make an order prohibiting the use of the dwelling house for human habitation until in their judgment it is rendered habitable. A new sub-section embodying important hygienic proposals is embodied in the clause. Quoted in extenso it runs thus:— "A room habitually used as a sleeping place, the surface of the floor of which is more than three feet below the surface of the part of the street adjoining or nearest to the room, shall for the purposes of this section be deemed to be so dangerous or injurious to health as to be unfit for human habitation, if the room either (a) is not sufficiently protected against dampness, effluvia, or exhalation; or (b) is not sufficiently ventilated; or (c) is not in every part thereof at least seven feet in height from floor to ceiling; or (d) is not, to the extent of one foot at least in height, above the level of the surface of the part of the street adjoining or nearest to the room; or (e) has not one or more windows opening directly into the external air, with a total area clear of the sash frames equal to at least one-tenth of the floor area of the room, and so constructed that one-half at least of each window of the room can be opened, the opening in each case extending to the top of the window; or (f) is not provided along the entire frontage thereof with an open area properly paved, at least four feet wide in every part thereof: Provided that in the area there may be placed steps necessary for access to the room, and over and across the area there may be steps necessary for access to any buildings above the room, if the steps are so placed in each case as not to be over or across any external window." ACCOMMODATION FOR COOKING FOOD IN TENEMENT HOUSES. London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1908. 7. If at any time it appears to any sanitary authority that in any tenement house within their district sufficient and suitable accommodation for the cooking of food is not provided for the use of each family occupying such house on the storey or one of the storeys in which are situate the rooms or lodgings in the separate occupation of such family such sanitary authority may cause notice to be served on the owner of such house requiring him within such reasonable time as may be specified in the notice to provide sufficient and suitable accommodation for the purpose aforesaid, and any owner failing to comply with any such requirement within the period prescribed in the notice shall be liable on summary conviction to a penalty not exceeding forty shillings and to a daily penalty not exceeding twenty shillings : Provided that this section shall not apply to any tenement house used or occupied as such before the passing of this Act. 170 SEWERAGE, DRAINAGE AND SEWER VENTILATION. Hutchings Wharf; Rigden Street; Sewer Ventilation, Proposed Shafts Northern Outfall; Flooding, Severe Storm. In the memorandum issued by the Local Government Board to the Medical Officers of Health respecting their Animal Reports, it is stated that the Local Government Board desired to obtain, through the Annual Reports of the Medical Officers of Health, information of particular changes of condition that occur by the action of the Local Authority, and one of the subject matters which is required to be reported upon is the condition of sewer and house drains. Hutchings Wharf. The Borough Surveyor reported that an old brick sewer at Hutchings Wharf, Hutchings Street, Millwall, was in a dilapidated condition, and practically had no fall. New buildings were in course of erection which it was proposed should be connected to this sewer. The Borough Surveyor recommended that a pipe sewer be constructed. Rigden Street. The Borough Surveyor recommended the opening up of Rigden Street sewer, and substitution of new pipes for the existing defective brick sewer which was reported to have collapsed in several places. Sewer Ventilation, Proposed Shafts Northern Outfall. The Public Health and Housing Committee on various occasions called the attention of the London County Council to the nuisance arising from the open surface ventilators to the northern outfall sewer, and urged that the construction of tall brick shafts over the ventilators should be undertaken. The London County Council Committee replied that they regretted they were unable to adopt the proposal. 171 The Public Health and Housing Committee were strongly of opinion steps should be taken by the London County Council to remedy the nuisance by constructing shafts as suggested, and recommended the Council that further representations be made to the London County Council, urging the construction of ventilating shafts as proposed, and the members representing the Borough on the County Council be requested to support such representations. The following letter was received from the London County Council:— London County Council, County Hall, Spring Gardens, S.W., 3rd December, 1908. Sir,—A Committee of the Council have had before them your letter of the 11th ultimo, containing the suggestion that tall brick shafts should be erected over the open ventilators to the northern outfall sewer. In reply, I am to say that the Committee, after carefully considering the proposal, regret that they are unable to see their way to adopt it. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, G. L. GOMME, Clerk to the Council. The Town Clerk of the Metropolitan Borough of Poplar. In view of the nuisance arising from the open ventilators in question the Public Health and Housing Committee directed the London County Council be again urged to construct shafts as proposed, similar to that already provided on the London County Council sewer in Devons Road. Flooding—Severe Storm. On the occasion of the storm on the evening of the 4th June, the basements of several premises in the Borough were temporarily flooded, due to the surcharge of the London County Council sewers, which prevented the local sewers discharging thereinto. The Borough Surveyor communicated with the Engineer of the London County Council on the matter, and the Engineer pointed out that the rainfall in Poplar on the 172 day in question was the most severe that had occurred in the records of the County Council, the actual gauge reading at the Isle of Dogs pumping station being 117 inches of rain in 30 minutes. He stated that it is quite impossible for any system of sewers to deal with such a downpour of rain in that time. COMBINED DRAINAGE. Reconstruction by Council. Metropolitan Sewers and Drains Bill. At the Conference of representatives of the City and Metropolitan Borough Councils, held at the St. Pancras Town Hall on the 24th June, 1907, it was referred to a Committee of the Conference to prepare, for presentation to the President of the Local Government Board, a Petition or Draft Bill dealing with the subject of combined drainage. The Executive Committee, comprising delegates representing Battersea, Fulham, Greenwich, Hackney, Kensington, Lambeth, Paddington, St. Pancras and Woolwich, met on several occasions at the Westminister City Hall, through the kind permission of the Town Clerk, and drafted a short Bill. A second conference of the City and Metropolitan Borough Councils was then held at the St. Pancras Town Hall, on the 6th December, 1907, to receive the Committee's report and Draft Bill. On the 31st January, 1908, Mr. W. H. Dickinson, the Member of 173 Parliament for North St. Pancras, was approached, and was asked to take charge of the Bill and to bring it before the House of Commons. Mr. Dickinson kindly replied on the 1st February that he would be very happy to take charge of the Bill. The Bill was introduced into the House of Commons on the 5th March by Mr. Dickinson and was read a first time. The Bill is set out below in extenso:— METROPOLITAN SEWERS AND DRAINS BILL. MEMORANDUM. The object of this Bill is to amend the state of the law relating to combined drainage. There are two kinds of combined drain, the "combined drain" which in law is a "drain," and the "combined drain" which in law is a "sewer." The former is repairable by the private owner; the latter by the public authority. In the opinion of builders, owners, and local authorities, combined drains were originally constructed as drains, and were mutually regarded as such until the retrospective case of Travis v. Uttley (1893), the effect of which has been to convert thousands of drains, which had been privately owned and privately maintained, into sewers vested in the local authorities, and maintainable at the expense of the ratepayers. The state of the law has been described by the Lord Chief Justice, who said in a recent decision, "It seems to me, as I have before pointed out, to be essential that there should be some amendment of the law upon this subject. The state of the authorities is such that it appears to be impossible either to reconcile the various decisions or to deduce from them any logical rule." Several provincial authorities, notably West Ham, Croydon, Carlisle, Leyton, Wood Green, Sheffield, etc., have been successful in obtaining the powers which the Metropolitan local authorities ask for by this Bill. 174 [8 Edw. 7.] Metropolitan Sewers and Drains. A Bill to amend the Metropolis Management Acts with respect to Sewers and Drains. Be it enacted by the King's Most Excellent Majesty, by and with the advice and consent of the Lords Spiritual and Temporal, and Commons, in this present Parliament assembled, and by the authority of the same, as follows:— 1. In and for the purposes of the Metropolis Management Acts, 1855 to 1899, and the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, or any Act or Acts amending the same, the word "drain" shall be deemed to include any sewer or drain, whether constructed before or after the passing of this Act, with which two or more houses, buildings, or premises are, at the date of the passing of this Act, or may at any time thereafter be connected, or which is used or capable of being or intended to be used for the conveyance of the drainage of such houses, buildings, or premises directly or indirectly to any sewer which has been approved as such by the Metropolitan Commissioners of Sewers, the Metropolitan Board of Works, or the London County Council, but shall not include any sewer so approved as aforesaid. 2. This Act may be cited as the Metropolis Management Amendment Act, 1908, and shall be construed as one with the Metropolis Management Act, 1855, and the Acts amending the same. On the back of the Bill appear the following names:—Mr. Dickinson, Sir William Bull, Mr. Bowles, Sir William Collins, Mr. Crooks, Mr. Timothy Davies, Sir George Far dell, Mr. Claude Hay, Sir Henry Kimber, Earl Percy, Sir Albert Spicer, and Mr. Waterlow. The Boroughs represented are St. Pancras, Hammersmith, Lambeth Woolwich, Fulham, Paddington, Shoreditch, Wandsworth, Kensington, Hackney and Islington. Position of the Bill. The Bill was down for second reading every night in the last Session of Parliament from the 12th October to 21st December, but was not reached. The Bill has therefore been before the House since March 5th, 175 1908, and it seems that unless the Government take it up there is little prospect of the Bill becoming law. The Bill will be reintroduced during the coming Session, and I understand it is suggested to Mr. Dickinson, who has charge of the Bill, that strong representations should be made to the individual members of the Cabinet. Verminous Rooms* and Licensed Premises' Urinals. Verminous Rooms. Verminous rooms are still brought to the notice of owners upon the "Intimations" which are followed, if necessary, by "Statutory Notices," more especially as there are generally other defects upon the premises, and certainly verminous premises are " in such a state as to be a nuisance to health," and owners, if they do not already know, are informed when defects are being dealt with under the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, that the vermin must be got rid of, otherwise the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1904, will be brought into operation. When a notice is served for cleansing under the bye-laws for houses let in lodgings or occupied by members of more than one family, should the premises be verminous a special intimation to that effect is enclosed. The above procedure saves a large amount of time, as under the London County Council (General Powers) Act a certificate must be given by the Medical Officer of Health, who would have to inspect every room. However, eleven certificates had to be given to the Public Health and Housing Committee. During the year 213 rooms have been cleansed of vermin:— Poplar South Division 2 West Combined Division 24 East Combined Division 12 Bromley Central Division 15 Bromley North Division 50 Bow West Division 15 Bow East Division 23 Houses let in lodgings 72 No summonses were applied for. * For the Cleansing of Verminous Persons see page 87. 177 Licensed Premises' Urinals. Four notices were served under Section 22, calling upon the owners to provide proper doors to the urinals attached to their licensed premises. THE INCORPORATED SOCIETY FOR THE DESTRUCTION OF VERMIN. The Incorporated Society for the Destruction of Vermin desired that notice should be given them of cases of verminous dwellings in the Borough in order that they might, in conjunction with the sanitary officers, efficiently cleanse such places. It is intended to divide London into districts and allocate to each a number of men to strip and distemper walls, cleanse ceilings and floors, fumigate rooms and destroy verminous bedding (bedding, etc., destroyed being replaced by the Society). The men employed would be drawn from the unemployed registered by London Distress Committees and other bodies. The Society is a strong one influentially supported, and the Public Health and Housing Committee were of opinion every facility should be offered them to carry out the scheme, and recommended the Council that the Society for the Destruction of Vermin be notified of cases of verminous houses occurring in the Borough. Dear Sir,— I beg to inform you that this Council have had under consideration the question of the medical inspection of children in public elementary schools, under Section 13 of the Education (Administrative Provisions) Act, 1907, together with the Memorandum dated the 22nd November, 1907, issued by the Board of Education on the subject. This Council have received a report from their Public Health Committee upon the matter, and on their recommendation have arrived at the following conclusions with reference thereto: — (1) That it is desirable "on the grounds of efficiency, economy and harmony, that the work of medical inspection of school children under the provisions of the Education (Administrative Provisions) Act, should be carried out in intimate conjunction with public health authorities" as set out in paragraphs 5 and 6 of the Memorandum of the Board of Education, and that the Cities of London and Westminster and the Metropolitan Boroughs should for this purpose be made the units of administration. (2) That it is desirable that the Medical Officers of Health of the Cities of London and Westminster and the Metropolitan Boroughs be appointed (by the London County Council) as the local administrative officers, co-operating for this purpose with the Medical Officer of Health for the County of London. 179 (3) That it is desirable that any necessary executive assistants should be appointed by the London County Council and allocated to the Cities of London and Westminster and the Metropolitan Boroughs, such assistants to be under the control of, and to receive instructions from, and report directly to the Medical Officers of Health. (4) That it is desirable that the Medical Officers of Health should report directly to the London County Council on all matters relating to the administration of the Act. In accordance with the direction of this Council these conclusions have been communicated to the London County Council, and they were requested to adopt and incorporate the same in any scheme to be prepared by them under the Act referred to. The Board of Education were also communicated with. I am now directed to ask that the Council of the Metropolitan Borough of Poplar will be good enough to take the matter into consideration and that they will support the action of this Council. In one case where a w.c. pan and trap were fixed without notice being given under Clause 14 of the London County Council Bye-laws, Section 39 (1) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, the builder was written to asking him to open the ground, otherwise legal proceedings would be instituted against him for infringing the bye-law. This mode of procedure prevents good work being constructed on to defective work. Whenever drainage work has been found to have been done and covered up without inspection, if the builder will not open the ground, the work is exposed by the Sanitary Authority, and such a line of action ensures sound work. In 1904 the journals of the Sanitary Inspectors were examined from the date of the coming into operation of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, and lists were compiled (which involved a large amount of work) of houses not inspected, and also those inspected with the number of inspections made. Lists of houses not inspected were given to the Inspectors with instructions to inspect such premises as soon as possible. The inspection of these premises is now completed. The inspections took a long time as many defective drainage systems had been discovered, which had to be rectified before further inspections were made and fresh drainage work created. 181 List of Sanitary Works carried out in the Metropolitan Borough of Popular, from January 1st, 1908, to December 31st, 1908.* Poplar South. Mr. Foad. 36, Tidey Street Defective drain 30th December. 14 days' abatement order. 25/- costs. Mr. Langley. 188 SANITARY LEGISLATION. White Phosphorous Matches Prohibition Act, 1908. 8 Edw. 7, Cap. 42. " The object of this statute, which was introduced into the House of Commons by the Home Secretary, is to enable the Government to adhere to a convention which was approved at an International Conference on Labour Regulation, held at Berne in September, 1906. That convention —agreeing to prohibit the manufacture, importation, and sale of matches that contain white phosphorous—was signed at the time by the representatives of Germany, Denmark, France, Italy,. Luxembourg, the Netherlands, and Switzerland; but His Majesty's Government was not then able to agree to the prohibition, for reasons explained in a memorandum (Cd. 3271) laid before Parliament in 1906. "For the better protection from necrosis of persons employed in factories where white phosphorous is used, it has recently been found necessary either to impose regulations more stringent than those hitherto in force, or to prohibit the use of white phosphorous altogether. After consultation with manufacturers, the latter course has been adopted, and with it follows, likewise, the prohibition of importation of such matches. "White phosphorous is not used in the manufacture of matches which strike only on a prepared substance. Substitutes for white phosphorous in the manufacture of "strike-anywhere" matches are in use, but are patented. By arrangement with the holders of the only patents which, to the knowledge of the Home Office, are being used in the industry in the United Kingdom, Section 4 has been inserted in the Act, making those patents available, on reasonable terms, to all manufacturers." (Paterson's Practical Statutes.) Children Act, 1908. See also page (20). 8 Edw. 7, Cap. 67. "This statute, while to a large extent consolidating—for it repeals and re-enacts the whole of no less than twenty-one Acts, as well as great part of many others—at the same time effects important amendments in the existing law. It deals with the protection of infants, children, and 189 young persons, with the punishment of juvenile offenders, and with reformatory and industrial schools. "Part I., relating to infant life protection, is practically a re-enactment with amendments of the Infant Life Protection Act, 1897 (60 and 61 Vict., c. 57), which was itself in the nature of a consolidating statute. The most important changes made with respect to "baby-farming" are the application of the former provisions to the case of a single infant, and the extension of the age from five to seven years. Minor amendments are noticed in the notes to the sections. "Part II., relating to the prevention of cruelty to children and young persons, similarly re-enacts with amendments by far the greater part (though not the whole) of the Prevention of Cruelty to Children Act, 1904 (4 Edw. 7, c. 15). It also contains several new provisions, among which may be specially mentioned: a penalty on the suffocation of an infant under three by a drunken person in the same bed with it (Section 13); a penalty on exposing a child under seven to the risk of burning from an open fire (Section 15); a penalty on allowing children or young persons between four and sixteen to be in a brothel (Section 16); a penalty on causing, encouraging, or favouring the seduction or prostitution of a girl under sixteen (Section 17) ; a power to bind over a person having the custody of a girl under sixteen to exercise proper care (Section 18). Power is also given to the Home Secretary to inspect private charitable institutions for the reception of children or young persons. "Part III, relating to juvenile smoking, introduces some novel principles in a rather complicated fashion. It is made an offence to sell cigarettes (which includes tobacco) and cigarette papers to a person apparently under sixteen; and the duty is imposed upon constables and park-keepers to seize any cigarettes or cigarette papers in the possession of such young person found smoking in a public place, and to search him (if a boy). Special provision is made for the regulation of automatic machines for the sale of cigarettes. It may be observed here that cigars are apparently included, but pipes certainly not. "Part IV., relating to reformatory and industrial schools, is in the main a combined re enactment of the Reformatory Schools Act, 1866 (29 and 30 Vict., cap. 117), and the Industrial Schools Act, 1866 (29 and 30 Vict., c. 118), with the amendments introduced by subsequent legislation. The new provisions are neither numerous nor important. The only one that need be mentioned here is that (Section 74) which prescribes the duties and powers of local authorities with respect to the maintenance, etc., of inmates of certified schools. 190 "Part V., relating to juvenile offenders, is for the most part new though some of the provisions are re-enacted, with amendments, from the Youthful Offenders Act, 1901 (1 Edw. 7, c. 20). Special juvenile police courts are to be established, and also a system of registered places of detention for children and young persons. All juvenile offenders are, as a general rule, to be admitted to bail by the police, and are, in any case, to be kept from association with adults. The attendance at court of the parent or guardian is, as a general rule, to be required; and the parent or guardian may be ordered to pay damages or give security even when the juvenile offender is not himself convicted. No child (under fourteen) may be sentenced to penal servitude or imprisonment; nor may any young person (under sixteen) be sentenced to penal servitude or to imprisonment except when of an unruly or depraved character. Sentence of death is not to be pronounced against any child or young person, but for heinous crimes they may be sentenced to an indeterminate period of detention. "Part VI., contains some important miscellaneous provisions of a general nature. Power is given to clear the court when a child or young person is giving evidence in cases affecting decency or morality. No child or young person is allowed to be in court during the trial of other persons. Penalties are imposed on the purchase of old metals from a person under sixteen, on the taking of pawns from a person under fourteen, on vagrants preventing children from receiving education, and on any one giving intoxicating liquor to a child under five. Children (under fourteen) are to be excluded altogether from the bar of licensed premises. Special precautions must be taken for the safety of children at entertainments, when the number of children exceeds 100 and the accommodation for them is upstairs. Finally, there are provisions for the cleansing of verminous children who attend a public elementary school, and a penalty is imposed upon the parent, etc., who allows his child to require such cleansing a second time. "The Act comes into operation on 1st April, 1909." (Paterson's Practical Statutes.) (7448) Order of the Board op Agriculture and Fisheries. (Dated 23rd April, 1908.) Public Health Offices, Bow Road, E. Dtcember 31st, 1908. To the Medical Officer of Health. Sir,— I beg to report I have during the year ended December 31st, 1908, made the following inspections :— Primary Inspection of Premises :— On Complaints of Nuisances 863 On Notifications of Infectious Diseases 177 On House-to-House Inspection 353 1393 House-to-House Inspection in the Division was carried out in the following streets Rippoth Road. Baker's Alley. Comboss Road. Wyke Road. Rothbury Road. Monier Road. Eliza Place. Allanmouth Road. Smeed Road. L.C.C. Schools. Smoke Nuisances. Only one case of smoke nuisance occurred in the district arising from without the district. Where nuisances have occurred within the 200 district intimation and notice have been served and the nuisance abated thereon, with two exceptions necessitating recourse to legal proceedings. No smoke nuisance presented itself under the Highways and Locomotives Act. Canal Boats Acts and Regulations. Under the Canal Boats Acts and Regulations 25 boats have been examined. Of these 16 were registered and 9 non-registered. All the registered canal boats, with one exception, were found in fair condition and in compliance with the Acts and Regulations. In the case of one registered canal boat the name on boat was not sufficiently legible. An intimation to the owner was immediately complied with. No contravention of the Acts was discovered on any of the nonregistered barges. Demolitions. Three shops, Nos. 728, 730, 732, Old Ford Road, have been demolished and the site cleared for the erection of six cottages. Unsound Food. The following unsound foods were dealt with during the year.— Twelve cases and eleven half cases of eggs. Twenty-five tins of blown condensed milk. One bushel of apples. One box of skate wings. One box of plaice. One sack of cocoanuts. One case of rabbits. Each of these parcels of food were condemned and destroyed. One hundred and six 21b. boxes and 141bs. of loose figs unfit for food and exposed for sale in a public market place were seized and a condemnation order obtained and the figs destroyed, followed by the issuing of a summons against the vendor. At the police court hearing the defendant was committed for trial at Sessions. 201 Offensive Businesses. The new regulations governing offensive businesses came into force on November 25th, 1907. The fat melters in the district have been required to conduct their businesses strictly in conformity thereto. The necessary alterations to premises and plant are not yet completed. Verminous Booms. 47 verminous rooms have been cleansed under the provisions of the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1902. Workshops and Workplaces. The workshops and workplaces have been visited and found generally satisfactory, and any nuisances arising within these premises have been abated on the service of intimation. Homework. The outworkers, who are all boot and shoe operatives, have been visited twice during the year, and the premises of each examined for nuisances and sanitary defects. The workrooms were mostly found in a fair condition, and any defects in the premises were remedied by the owner or occupier on a notice being served. Milkshops and Cowsheds, Food Preparation, etc. The milkshops and cowsheds have given no cause for complaint; the cowsheds were limewhited and cleansed twice during the year, in accordance with the provisions of the Dairy and Cowsheds orders, and the milk vessels and measures of the various milk vendors were not unsatisfactory. The places where foodstuffs are prepared have been periodically visited. Ice Cream Makers and Vendors. On visiting the ice cream makers and vendors during the season, the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1902 was apparently care, fully complied with. Food and Drugs Acts. Under the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts fifty-two varied samples have been purchased and submitted to the Public Analyst for analyses. Four on analysis were found to be adulterated—one only sufficiently to require the vendor to be cautioned by the Public Health and Housing Committee, whilst in the cases of the remaining three, summonses were 202 issued against the vendors. Particulars of adulterated samples and results of proceedings are appended. Butter.—Containing 72.0 per cent, of foreign fat. Summons issued, but offender absconded before service was effected. Butter.—Adulterated with 78.9 per cent, of foreign fat. Defendant fined 40s. and 23s. costs. Milk.—Adulterated with 9.4 per cent, of water. Defendant fined 10s. and 23s. costs. Milk.—Deficient in fat and solids not fat to the extent of 3.3 and 3.5 per cent, respectively, indicating at least 3.5 per cent, of added water. Vendor cautioned. Seven samples were mixed with a foreign substance, i.e., boric acid (preservative), in varying small proportions, making in all twelve defective samples. Under your instructions 52 samples of food stuffs have been purchased and submitted to the Public Analyst. The undermentioned articles were bought, viz.:— Milk, 20; butter, 17; coffee, 2; cocoa, 3; margarine, 2; vinegar, 3; jallap, 1; pepper, 2; mustard, 1; arrowroot, 1. The result of the analyses of above samples showed that the articles were genuine. Factories, Workshops, Workplaces and Outworkers. During the year the premises upon the Register have been periodically inspected, and where necessary, for sanitary purposes, notices have been duly served upon the responsible persons, and the nuisances were abated thereon. Milk Shops. There are 48 milk shops in the district and these were inspected periodically. On a few occasions the shop keeper had to be cautioned about the cleanliness of the milk vessels and utensils. The shop keepers are all duly registered by name. 208 Cowhouses and Dairies. There are three cowhouses in the district and these are thoroughly inspected at least four times a year, to see that the cleansing is carried out, which is done without any trouble. Frequent inspections are also paid to see that the hands of the milkers are clean during the operation; I find that in some of the sheds a pail of warm water is provided for this purpose. Slaughter Houses. There are four licensed slaughter houses in the district. These are periodically inspected, and are kept in a satisfactory condition. Only small animals are allowed to be killed in these registered premises. Ofttimes a visit is made while slaughtering is in progress. Eating Houses. These were inspected and found satisfactory. Included under this heading are seven fried fish shops, which are visited for the purpose of inspecting the class of fish used in the trade and sold to the public. Bake-Houses. There are 20 bake-houses in the district, and all are regularly inspected. The work of cleansing is carried out satisfactorily. Hairdressers' Shops. There are 11 of these shops upon the register and they are visited frequently. The utensils used in this business are kept clean. Ice Cream Shops. There are many of these shops throughout the district, but on a small seale; the owners only supply the ice cream during the summer months, and during that period visits are often made and the utensils inspected. The place where the ice cream is stored over night is particularly investigated. Markets and Stalls. Much attention has been paid to these with a view of preventing the sale of unsound food. Night inspections have also been made, and good results have been obtained. The class of meat sold has, in my opinion, been sound and fit for human food. 209 Unsound Food. During the year varied assortments of food stuffs have been surrendered by the owners as being unfit for food. Certificates were given where the articles were destroyed. On Thursday, 28th June, 1908, 1 ton 2cwt. of beef was seized and taken before the Magistrate, who condemned it at once, and on 3rd July, 1908, two men, who were concerned in the preparation for sale of this meat, were fined £20 and 5 guineas costs and £15 and 5 guineas costs respectively. During the month of December, 1908, unsound food was seen upon a stall in the Stepney district, and the Medical Officer of Health for that Borough, acting upon the information, visited the stall and seized a carcase of unsound beef. Smoke Nuisances. Many smoke nuisances have been notified, but only in one instance has the help of the Police Court been sought to abate the said nuisance. Offensive Matter. There is a great improvement in the manner in which offensive material is conveyed through the district. Only on two occasions were carriers cautioned, but the nuisances were very slight. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, H. J. LANGLEY, Assoc. San. Inst. Cert. Sanitary Inspector Exam. Schools. The whole of the schools, which comprise the following:— Oban Street Council Schools, Bromley Hall Road Council Schools, Hay Currie Council Schools, (Entirely new, late Byron and Bright Street Schools), St. Leonard's Road Council Schools, Marner Street Council Schools, were inspected and found in fair sanitary condition; the drinking water supplies were drawn direct from Metropolitan Water Board mains; the storage tanks were periodically cleansed, only used for supplies to lavatories and closets. Inspection of Bakehouses, etc. I have, as instructed, made the periodical inspections of those (13) premises (three not in use), and, where required, served Extracts of Factory and Workshop Act, 1891, "intimations" and statutory notices for the necessary work of cleansing and repairs, which were respectively complied with. Hairdressers' Shops. The whole of the hairdressers' shops in division were inspected with respect to the cleanliness of the utensils used in the business and sanitary conditions generally of the premises. Where defects existed, "intimations" and statutory notices were served and nuisances abated. Public-House Urinals, etc. Attention has been given to the urinals that they were kept as clean as possible and furnished with the necessary fittings in compliance with the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1904. Verminous Booms. During the course of year 15 rooms were cleansed; it has not been P 214 necessary to enforce the provisions of the London County Council's (General Powers) Act, 1904. Eating Houses, Coffee Shops, Pork Butchers, Sausage Makers, Fried Fish Shops. Inspections were several times made of the whole of the abovedescribed business premises and all necessary works of cleansing and repairs carried out, in some cases without service of "Intimations," etc. In no instance upon such visits was there cause for complaint so far as the condition of food. Cowsheds, Milk Sellers and Purveyors. The above (51) premises were frequently visited, the Public Health, etc., Office Registers corrected in the cases of those found non-registered through change of occupancy. The new proprietors were supplied with forms which were duly signed for the required alteration. Constant attention has been given to the enforcement of regulations relating to cleanliness of utensils, more particularly in compliance with the new London County Council (General Powers) Act relating to the sanitary conditions and surroundings under which milk was sold. Upon notice received for renewal of the licence of cowshed at 27, Abbott Road, the premises were again inspected, found in fair sanitary condition, consent was given to the application of the owner of this business to the London County Council. Examination of Food. I have on several occasions, upon applications made by different itinerant vendors and tradesmen, examined articles of food purchased by them at the markets, and when found "unsound, unwholesome and unfit for food," witnessed the same destroyed by the owners, certificates having been given to that effect. In no case was there any article of food "exposed for sale, deposited for sale, or preparation for sale" at the time of my visit to any premises in such condition as to justify seizure. Ice-Cream Makers and Vendors. All the premises where ice-creams have been made were visited, with 217 the object of detecting nuisances and any infringement of the London Council General Powers Act, 1902. The barrows were found to be legibly marked with names and addresses, etc., of vendors, also utensils, and the premises in question were clean and the ice-creams were wholesome. Factory and Workshops Inspections. Nuisances arising within the several factories, workshops, workplaces, etc., were dealt with. The whole of the ascertained business premises above described were inspected and found, in the majority of cases, to be in fair sanitary condition. In the remainder the work of repairs, cleansing, etc., has been carried out in compliance with "intimations," notices, etc. Smoke Nuisances. I have, where a nuisance has occurred from smoke emitted from any chimney other than belonging to a private dwelling-house, viz., factories, workshops, workplaces, etc., either on complaint or from my own personal observation, and in most cases the nuisances complained of were abated on the service of "intimations," and when necessary followed by statutory notices. In only one instance was it necessary to resort to legal proceedings against a firm in St. Leonard's Street, which resulted in a penalty of 40s. and 23s. costs inflicted for smoke nuisance. Canal Boats. I have inspected 39 barges, 38 non-registered and one registered Bailing barge, with the result that I have found the cabins fairly clean, and no infringement of regulations discovered. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, RICHARD E. Sir,— I beg to lay before you my eighth Annual Report of the Sanitary work and other duties carried out under the different Acts in connection therewith in the West Combined District during the past year, rendering as far as possible a detailed account of nuisances abated, inspections made, summonses applied for, etc. Inspection of Division. In investigating complaints and making inspections of all kinds 1328 houses or other premises have been visited, 287 of these visits being on account of notifications of infectious diseases. This number shows a satisfactory decrease of 105 cases upon those of the previous year. Of the 287 cases 13 were notified from the Poplar and Stepney Sick Asylum, 9 from Langley House Receiving Home and 4 from the Poplar Union. House to House Inspections. These visits to the number of 132 were carried out in the undermentioned places, a large number of the drains proving unsound (some were very bad indeed) on being tested :— Canton Street. Woollett Street, Nos. 17, 18, 19, 20, 21 (part), 22, 23, 24, 25, 26, 27, 28. Turners Buildings, Nos. 1, 2, 3. Upper North Street, Nos. 22, 35, 37, 40. Coffee and Eating Houses. These have been periodically inspected during the year, alterations in tenancy and new shops have been registered and the registers brought up to date. While inspecting these places, so far as possible attention has been paid to the food in preparation for cooking. They have been kept in fairly clean condition. Food Inspection at Wharf, etc. At Aberdeen Wharf, where food, skins, and potted meats are landed for transit, inspections have been made from time to time and everything found in good condition. The new regulations (as to food) of the Local Government Board will now make more inspections at this wharf imperative, and care will be exercised in working in conjunction with the Customs Officers in the case of any food being discovered which is unsound and dealing with the same under these regulations. The butchers' premises and stalls in Chrisp Street Market are 221 inspected at night times at week ends and sometimes on Sunday mornings. In each place where sausages and brawn are made special attention has been given. These places are conducted in a clean and careful manner, and are regularly cleansed. Several consignments of damaged wheat, rice, barley, etc., have been received into the district from the "docks" to be made up into poultry food. These have been watched through and dealt with. The following is a list of food destroyed during the year :— 1 leg of pork, 1 lump of bacon, 1 box of plaice, 1 box of tomatoes, 2 sacks of potatoes, 1 barrel of herrings, and many lots of damaged fruit in small quantities. Factory and Workshops. All those now on the register are regularly visited. "Notices" for cleansing have been served and complied with as required. No case of overcrowding or infectious disease in any workshop has been found to exist. Outworkers. The premises where male outworkers live have been visited at intervals. Changes of address have been noted and the registers altered accordingly. "Notices" to cleanse the rooms in some cases have been served and attended to. Bakehouses. The occupied bakehouses now number 25 and these have been regularly inspected during the year. The bi-annual cleansing has been carried out satisfactorily and the places conducted in a cleanly manner, everything at all times of visits being in order. 222 Dairies, Cowsheds, Milkshops and Slaughterhouses. These places are regularly visited during the year. The dippers and storage vessels have been closely inspected as to cleanliness and have, almost without exception, been kept in a good condition and the milkpans have been kept covered over to keep out dust, etc. In some cases where paraffin oil is sold it has been removed to a further distance from the milkpans, and in other cases to the rear of the premises. In three or four cases the sale has been stopped. All changes of tenancy in milkshops (which are of frequent occurrence) have been noted and the registers altered accordingly and brought up to date. The number of these on the registers at present are as follows:— Milkshops 72 Cowshed 1 Slaughterhouses 2 Canal Boats Acts. Canal boats that are "registered" under the Acts are visited as opportunities offer. They have been found, as regards the cabins where the occupants dwell, in an exceptionally clean and bright condition, and the cubic space allowed has been utilised to the fullest extent. The means of ventilation has been kept in order, and no cases of illness have been discovered. The "papers" have been produced and examined at times, and no overcrowding has occurred. Smoke Nuisances. All complaints from the London County Council have been dealt with and also my own observations. No legal proceedings were instituted as the " notices" which were served were immediately attended to. Food and Drugs Acts, 1875 to 1907. During the year 56 samples of various kinds have been purchased for analysis, 7 of which proved to be adulterated badly. 223 Legal proceedings were instituted in each case. In some of the others different preservatives were found, and slight adulteration, causing three letters of caution to be sent to the vendors. In one case a summons was taken out for displaying an improper advertisement of margarine, contrary to the Act of 1907. Also in one case costs of £2 2s. were allowed against the Council, as the third portion of a sample was submitted to Somerset House, and the result proved different to the Public Analyst's certificate. Summons withdrawn, and £2 2s. costs allowed defendant. Butter.—Adulterated with 76.6 per cent. of foreign fat. Second offence. Defendant fined £10 and 23s. costs. Milk.—Deficient in fat to extent of 15 per cent. Purchased from itinerant vendor, previous convictions proved. Defendant Company fined £5 and 23s. costs. Butter.—Adulterated with 76 7 per cent. of foreign fat. Defendant fined £4 including costs. Butter.—Adulterated with 75.4 per cent. of foreign fat. Summons withdrawn on payment of £2 2s. costs by defendant. (By direction of Committee.) Margarine. —Improperly advertising. Summons withdrawn on payment of £2 2s. by defendant (same defendant as above). (By direction of Committee.) Milk.—Deficient in fat to extent of 8 3 per cent., purchased from itinerant vendor (third offence). Summons dismissed on production of warranty. Milk.—Deficient in solids not fat to extent of 8.2 per cent., showing an addition of 8.2 per cent. of added water. Defendant fined £2 and 23s. costs. 224 Milk.—Deficient in fat to extent of 46.6 per cent. Defendant fined £2 and 23s. costs. Public Health (London) Act, 1891. All cases of infectious diseases notified during the year have been followed up in order to trace their origin if possible. One summons was taken out under the above Act against the owner of premises which had been empty a long time, and which were reoccupied after alterations had been carried out. There was no water supply to the premises, and the house had been occupied for about three months. The owner lived in the country, and the local police failed to serve the summons properly. The owner then in the meantime had the water put on, and no further action could be taken with success. Metropolis Local Management Acts, 1904. One summons under this Act was taken out against the owner of 11 houses in respect of improper drainage. The owner claimed the main drain as a "sewer," and the case went to Court. An abatement order with 1s. penalty was obtained and 23s. costs, and the work was then proceeded with. London County Council (General Powers) Act. Under this Act about 24 rooms have been successfully freed from vermin. No legal proceedings were necessary. The public-house urinals, as far as possible, have been kept in a cleanly state. Ice-Cream Regulations. All premises where ice-cream is made have been inspected and attention given to all the utensils used. In only one case was there ecessity for threatening proceedings before the cleansing was carried out. I am, Sir, Your obedient Servant, JOSEPH JOHNSON, E.P.C., Cert. San. Insp. Exam. Sir,— I beg to submit to you my thirteenth Annual Report of the sanitary work carried out in my district (East Combined Division) during the twelve months ended 31st December, 1908. Inspection of Division. 1587 premises have been inspected, 1288 in response to complaints of nuisances, 137 on account of notifications of infectious diseases, and the remainder 162 house-to-house visitations. Of the 1587 premises so dealt with 921 were found to be in a fair sanitary condition, whilst the remainder, 666 premises, nuisances were found to exist, which necessitated the following sanitary works being executed (see tabulated list, page 181).
